<p><i><b>Opportunity</b></i>, also known as <b>MER-B</b> (<b>Mars Exploration Rover – B</b>) or <b>MER-1</b>, is a robotic <a href="/wiki/Rover_(space_exploration)" title="Rover (space exploration)">rover</a> active on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> since 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-Opportunity_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-Opportunity-1">[1]</a></sup> Launched on July 7, 2003 as part of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover" title="Mars Exploration Rover">Mars Exploration Rover</a> program, it landed in <a href="/wiki/Meridiani_Planum" title="Meridiani Planum">Meridiani Planum</a> on January 25, 2004, three weeks after its twin <i><a href="/wiki/Spirit_(rover)" title="Spirit (rover)">Spirit</a></i> (MER-A) touched down on the other side of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-january_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-january-7">[7]</a></sup> With a planned 90 <a href="/wiki/Timekeeping_on_Mars#Sols" title="Timekeeping on Mars">sol</a> duration of activity, <i>Spirit</i> functioned until getting stuck in 2009 and ceased communications in 2010, while <i>Opportunity</i> remains active as of January 11, 2017, having already exceeded its operating plan by <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008401155200000000♠</span>12 years, 260 days (in Earth time). <i>Opportunity</i> has continued to move, gather scientific observations, and report back to Earth for over 50 times its designed lifespan. As of October 4, 2016, the rover had traveled 26.99 miles (43.44 km). This date was mission time of Sol (Mars days) 4514. For latest updates see bottom of page <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_mission_timeline" title="Opportunity mission timeline"><i>Opportunity</i> mission timeline</a></p>
<p>Mission highlights include the initial 90 sol mission, finding extramartian <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Heat_Shield_Rock" title="Heat Shield Rock">Heat Shield Rock</a> (Meridiani Planum meteorite), and over two years studying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_crater" title="Victoria crater">Victoria crater</a>. It survived dust-storms and reached <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endeavour_crater" title="Endeavour crater">Endeavour crater</a> in 2011, which has been described as a "second landing site".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wustl_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-wustl-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a> (JPL), a division of the <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pasadena,_California" title="Pasadena, California">Pasadena, California</a>, manages the Mars Exploration Rover project for NASA's <a href="/wiki/Science_Mission_Directorate" title="Science Mission Directorate">Science Mission Directorate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Objectives">Objectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Opportunity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Objectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mer-b-final-launch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2023" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Mer-b-final-launch.jpg/220px-Mer-b-final-launch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Mer-b-final-launch.jpg/330px-Mer-b-final-launch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Mer-b-final-launch.jpg/440px-Mer-b-final-launch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mer-b-final-launch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Delta II Heavy (7925H-9.5) lifting off from pad 17-B carrying MER-B in 2003 with <i>Opportunity</i> rover.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The scientific objectives of the Mars Exploration Rover mission are to:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa1_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa1-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Search for and characterize a variety of rocks and soils that hold clues to past water activity. In particular, samples sought will include those that have minerals deposited by water-related processes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">evaporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary cementation</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_activity" title="Hydrothermal activity">hydrothermal activity</a>.</li>
<li>Determine the distribution and composition of minerals, rocks, and soils surrounding the landing sites.</li>
<li>Determine what <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">geologic processes</a> have shaped the local terrain and influenced the chemistry. Such processes could include water or wind erosion, sedimentation, hydrothermal mechanisms, volcanism, and cratering.</li>
<li>Perform calibration and validation of surface observations made by <a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</a> instruments. This will help determine the accuracy and effectiveness of various instruments that survey <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">Martian geology</a> from orbit.</li>
<li>Search for iron-containing minerals, identify and quantify relative amounts of specific mineral types that contain water or were formed in water, such as iron-bearing carbonates.</li>
<li>Characterize the <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a> and textures of rocks and soils and determine the processes that created them.</li>
<li>Search for geological clues to the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental conditions</a> that existed when liquid water was present.</li>
<li>Assess whether those environments were conducive to life.</li>
</ul>
<p>During the next two decades, NASA will continue to conduct missions to address whether life ever arose on Mars. The search begins with determining whether the Martian environment was ever suitable for life. Life, as we understand it, requires water, so the history of <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">water on Mars</a> is critical to finding out if the Martian environment was ever conducive to life. Although the Mars Exploration Rovers do not have the ability to detect life directly, they are offering very important information on the habitability of the environment in the planet's history.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_overview">Mission overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Opportunity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mission overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MOLA_opportunity.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/MOLA_opportunity.jpg/200px-MOLA_opportunity.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/MOLA_opportunity.jpg/300px-MOLA_opportunity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/MOLA_opportunity.jpg/400px-MOLA_opportunity.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MOLA_opportunity.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Opportunity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s landing site (denoted with a star).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA05229_label.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1084" data-file-width="1446" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/PIA05229_label.jpg/200px-PIA05229_label.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/PIA05229_label.jpg/300px-PIA05229_label.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/PIA05229_label.jpg/400px-PIA05229_label.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA05229_label.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mars Global Surveyor <a href="/wiki/Orbiter" title="Orbiter">orbiter</a>'s photograph of landing site showing "<a href="/wiki/Eagle_(crater)" title="Eagle (crater)">hole in one</a>." (See also: <a href="/wiki/File:Opportunity-02.gif" title="File:Opportunity-02.gif">simulation of <i>Opportunity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> trajectory on arrival at Mars in January 2004</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_mission_timeline" title="Opportunity mission timeline">Opportunity mission timeline</a></div>
<p>The primary surface mission for <i>Opportunity</i> was planned to last 90 <a href="/wiki/Timekeeping_on_Mars" title="Timekeeping on Mars">sols</a> (92 Earth days). The mission has received several extensions and has been operating for 4735 days since landing. An archive of weekly updates on the rover's status can be found at the <i>Opportunity</i> Update Archive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OpportunityUpdateArchive_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-OpportunityUpdateArchive-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>From its initial landing, by chance, into an <a href="/wiki/Eagle_(crater)" title="Eagle (crater)">impact crater</a> amidst an otherwise generally flat plain, <i>Opportunity</i> has successfully investigated soil and rock samples and taken panoramic photos of its landing site. Its sampling allowed NASA scientists to make hypotheses concerning the presence of <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a> and past presence of water on the surface of Mars. Following this, it was directed to travel across the surface of Mars to investigate another crater site, <a href="/wiki/Endurance_(crater)" title="Endurance (crater)">Endurance crater</a>, which it investigated from June – December 2004. Subsequently, <i>Opportunity</i> examined the impact site of its own <a href="/wiki/Heat_shield" title="Heat shield">heat shield</a> and discovered an intact <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorite</a>, now known as <a href="/wiki/Heat_Shield_Rock" title="Heat Shield Rock">Heat Shield Rock</a>, on the surface of Mars.</p>
<p>From late April 2005 to early June of that year, <i>Opportunity</i> was perilously lodged in a sand dune, with several wheels buried in the sand. Over a six-week period Earth-based physical simulations were performed to decide how best to extract the rover from its position without risking a permanent immobilization of the valuable vehicle. Successful maneuvering a few centimeters at a time eventually freed the rover, which resumed its travels.</p>
<p><i>Opportunity</i> was directed to proceed in a southerly direction to <a href="/wiki/Erebus_(crater)" title="Erebus (crater)">Erebus crater</a>, a large, shallow, partially buried crater and a stopover on the way south towards <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(crater)" title="Victoria (crater)">Victoria crater</a>, between October 2005 and March 2006. It experienced some mechanical problems with its robotic arm.</p>
<p>In late September 2006, <i>Opportunity</i> reached Victoria crater and explored along the rim in a clockwise direction. In June 2007 it returned to Duck Bay, its original arrival point; in September 2007 it entered the crater to begin a detailed study. In August 2008, <i>Opportunity</i> left Victoria crater for <a href="/wiki/Endeavour_(crater)" title="Endeavour (crater)">Endeavour crater</a>, which it reached on August 9, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_-_NASA_Mars_Rover_Arrives_at_New_Site_on_Martian_Surface_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_-_NASA_Mars_Rover_Arrives_at_New_Site_on_Martian_Surface-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:652px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MERB_Sol2710_1merbarrives.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1235" height="553" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/MERB_Sol2710_1merbarrives.jpg/650px-MERB_Sol2710_1merbarrives.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/MERB_Sol2710_1merbarrives.jpg/975px-MERB_Sol2710_1merbarrives.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f3/MERB_Sol2710_1merbarrives.jpg 2x" width="650"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MERB_Sol2710_1merbarrives.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Opportunity</i> arrives at Endeavour crater</div>
</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>Here at the rim of the Endeavour crater the rover moved around a geographic feature named <i>Cape York</i>. The Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter had detected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phyllosilicate" title="Phyllosilicate">phyllosilicates</a> there, and the rover analyzed the rocks with its instruments to check this sighting on the ground. This structure was analyzed in depth until summer 2013. At May 2013 the rover was heading south to a hill named <i><a href="/wiki/Solander_Point" title="Solander Point">Solander Point</a></i>.</p>
<p><i>Opportunity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s total odometry as of June 27, 2016 (sol 4417) was 42.85 km (26.63 mi), while the dust factor is 0.725. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Since January 2013, the solar array dust factor (one of the determinants of solar power production) varied from a relatively dusty 0.467 on December 5, 2013 (sol 3507) to a relatively clean 0.964 on May 13, 2014 (sol 3662).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Opportunity_Updates_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Opportunity_Updates-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2014, NASA reported that <i>Opportunity</i> was suffering from "<a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a>" events in which the rover fails to write data, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a> information, to <a href="/wiki/Non-volatile_memory" title="Non-volatile memory">non-volatile memory</a>. The hardware failure is believed to be due to an age-related fault in one of the rover's seven memory banks. As a result, NASA had aimed to force the rover's software to ignore the failed memory bank,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> however amnesia events continued to occur which eventually resulted in vehicle resets. In light of this, on Sol 4027 (May 23, 2015), the rover was configured to operate in RAM-only mode, completely avoiding the use of non-volatile memory for storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of January 11, 2017, <i>Opportunity</i> is still roving the surface of Mars, and slated for further scientific exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_and_construction">Design and construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Opportunity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Design and construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover" title="Mars Exploration Rover">Mars Exploration Rover</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_embedded_computer_systems_on_board_the_Mars_rovers" title="Comparison of embedded computer systems on board the Mars rovers">Comparison of embedded computer systems on board the Mars rovers</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MER_Pancam.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2594" data-file-width="1725" height="376" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/MER_Pancam.JPG/250px-MER_Pancam.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/MER_Pancam.JPG/375px-MER_Pancam.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/MER_Pancam.JPG/500px-MER_Pancam.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MER_Pancam.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pancam Mast Assembly (PMA)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Opportunity</i> (along with its twin, <i>Spirit</i>) is a six-wheeled, <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar-powered</a> robot standing 1.5 meters (4.9 ft) high, 2.3 meters (7.5 ft) wide, and 1.6 meters (5.2 ft) long and weighing 180 kilograms (400 lb). Six wheels on a <a href="/wiki/Rocker-bogie" title="Rocker-bogie">rocker-bogie</a> system enable mobility. Each wheel has its own motor, the vehicle is steered at front and rear and is designed to operate safely at tilts of up to 30 degrees. Maximum speed is 5 centimeters per second (2.0 in/s) although average speed is about a fifth of this (0.89 centimeters per second (0.35 in/s)). Both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_rover" title="Spirit rover"><i>Spirit</i></a> and <i>Opportunity</i> have pieces of the fallen <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center</a>'s metal on them that were "turned into shields to protect cables on the drilling mechanisms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martian_Robots.2C_Taking_Orders_From_a_Manhattan_Walk-Up_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martian_Robots.2C_Taking_Orders_From_a_Manhattan_Walk-Up-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roving_Mars:_Spirit.2C_Opportunity.2C_and_the_Exploration_of_the_Red_Planet_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roving_Mars:_Spirit.2C_Opportunity.2C_and_the_Exploration_of_the_Red_Planet-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Solar arrays generate about 140 watts for up to four hours per Martian day (sol) while rechargeable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium_ion_batteries" title="Lithium ion batteries">lithium ion batteries</a> store energy for use at night. <i>Opportunity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s onboard computer uses a 20 MHz <a href="/wiki/IBM_RAD6000" title="IBM RAD6000">RAD6000</a> CPU with 128 MB of DRAM, 3 MB of EEPROM, and 256 MB of flash memory. The rover's <a href="/wiki/Operating_temperature" title="Operating temperature">operating temperature</a> ranges from −40 to +40 °C (−40 to 104 °F) and <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_heater_unit" title="Radioisotope heater unit">radioisotope heaters</a> provide a base level of heating, assisted by electrical heaters when necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> A gold film and a layer of silica <a href="/wiki/Aerogel" title="Aerogel">aerogel</a> provide insulation.</p>
<p>Communications depends on an omnidirectional low-gain antenna communicating at a low data rate and a steerable high-gain antenna, both in direct contact with Earth. A low gain antenna is also used to relay data to spacecraft orbiting Mars.</p>
<p>Fixed science/engineering instruments include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pancam" title="Pancam">Panoramic Camera (Pancam)</a> – examines the texture, color, mineralogy, and structure of the local terrain.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Navcam" title="Navcam">Navigation Camera (Navcam)</a> – monochrome with a higher field of view but lower resolution, for navigation and driving.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mini-TES" title="Mini-TES">Miniature Thermal Emission Spectrometer (Mini-TES)</a> – identifies promising rocks and soils for closer examination, and determines the processes that formed them.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hazcam" title="Hazcam">Hazcams</a>, two B&amp;W cameras with 120 degree field of view, that provide additional data about the rover's surroundings.</li>
</ul>
<p>The rover arm holds the following instruments:</p>
<ul>
<li>Mössbauer spectrometer (MB) <a href="/wiki/MIMOS_II" title="MIMOS II">MIMOS II</a> – used for close-up investigations of the mineralogy of iron-bearing rocks and soils.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle_X-ray_spectrometer" title="Alpha particle X-ray spectrometer">Alpha particle X-ray spectrometer</a> (APXS) – close-up analysis of the abundances of elements that make up rocks and soils.</li>
<li>Magnets – for collecting magnetic dust particles</li>
<li>Microscopic Imager (MI) – obtains close-up, high-resolution images of rocks and soils.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rock_Abrasion_Tool" title="Rock Abrasion Tool">Rock Abrasion Tool (RAT)</a> – exposes fresh material for examination by instruments on board.</li>
</ul>
<p>The cameras produce 1024-pixel by 1024-pixel images, the data is compressed with <a href="/wiki/ICER" title="ICER">ICER</a>, stored, and transmitted later.</p>
<p>The rover's name was chosen through a <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover#Naming_of_Spirit_and_Opportunity" title="Mars Exploration Rover">NASA sponsored student essay competition</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power">Power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Opportunity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover#Power_and_electronic_systems" title="Mars Exploration Rover">Mars_Exploration_Rover § Power_and_electronic_systems</a></div>
<p>Examples of watt-hours per sol collected by the rover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OpportunityStatusUpdates_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-OpportunityStatusUpdates-20">[20]</a></sup> The rover uses combination of solar cells and a rechargeable chemical battery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars.nasa.gov_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-mars.nasa.gov-21">[21]</a></sup> This class of rover has two rechargeable lithium batteries with 8 amp-hour capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars.nasa.gov_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-mars.nasa.gov-21">[21]</a></sup> At the start of the mission the solar panels could provide up to around 900 watt-hours to recharge the battery and power system in one Sol, but this can vary due to a variety of factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars.nasa.gov_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-mars.nasa.gov-21">[21]</a></sup> In Eagle crater the cells were producing about 840 watt-hours, but by Sol 319 in December 2004, it had dropped to 730 watt-hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because Mars takes longer than the Earth to orbit the Sun, there is a seasonal variation that reduces sunlight during Winter. This occurs roughly once every 2 Earth years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">[23]</a></sup> By 2016, MER-B had endured seven Martian winters, during which times power levels drop which can mean the rover avoids doing activities that use a lot of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">[23]</a></sup> During its first winter power levels dropped to under 300 watt-hours per day for two months, but some later winters were not as bad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another factor that can reduce received power is dust in the atmosphere, especially dust storms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys.org_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-phys.org-24">[24]</a></sup> Dust storms have occurred most frequently when Mars is closest to the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys.org_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-phys.org-24">[24]</a></sup> Global dust storms in 2007 reduced power levels for Opportunity and Spirit so much they could only run for a few minutes each day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys.org_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-phys.org-24">[24]</a></sup> It is predicted that dust storms will hit later in 2016 also.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys.org_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-phys.org-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="We are past 2016 now, did the dust storms occur as predicted? (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Opportunity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Opportunity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s launch was managed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA%27s_Launch_Services_Program" title="NASA's Launch Services Program">NASA's Launch Services Program</a>. This was the first launch of the <a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a> Heavy. The launch period went from June 25 to July 15, 2003. The first launch attempt occurred on June 28, 2003, but the spacecraft launched nine days later on July 7, 2003 due to delays for range safety and winds, then later to replace items on the rocket (insulation and a battery). Each day had two instantaneous launch opportunities. On the day of launch, the launch was delayed to the second opportunity (11:18 p.m. EDT) in order to fix a valve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_findings">Scientific findings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Opportunity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Scientific findings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scientific_information_from_the_Mars_Exploration_Rover_mission" title="Scientific information from the Mars Exploration Rover mission">Scientific information from the Mars Exploration Rover mission</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA13708westernrimgeo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7854" data-file-width="4023" height="430" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fc/PIA13708westernrimgeo.jpg/220px-PIA13708westernrimgeo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fc/PIA13708westernrimgeo.jpg/330px-PIA13708westernrimgeo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fc/PIA13708westernrimgeo.jpg/440px-PIA13708westernrimgeo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA13708westernrimgeo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
MER-B has studied the Western rim since 2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Opportunity</i> has provided substantial evidence in support of the mission's primary scientific goals: to search for and characterize a wide range of <a href="/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Opportunity" title="List of rocks on Mars">rocks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martian_soil" title="Martian soil">soils</a> that hold clues to past <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">water activity on Mars</a>. In addition to investigating the water, <i>Opportunity</i> has also obtained astronomical observations and atmospheric data.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors">Honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Opportunity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Driving_Distances_on_Mars_and_the_Moon.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3438" data-file-width="1948" height="353" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Driving_Distances_on_Mars_and_the_Moon.png/200px-Driving_Distances_on_Mars_and_the_Moon.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Driving_Distances_on_Mars_and_the_Moon.png/300px-Driving_Distances_on_Mars_and_the_Moon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Driving_Distances_on_Mars_and_the_Moon.png/400px-Driving_Distances_on_Mars_and_the_Moon.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Driving_Distances_on_Mars_and_the_Moon.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Opportunity</i> rover - Driving Distance "Off-World" Record (<a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>; July 28, 2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140728a_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140728a-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRB-20140729_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FRB-20140729-27">[27]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Honoring <i>Opportunity'</i>s great contribution to the exploration of Mars, an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> was named Opportunity— <a href="/wiki/39382_Opportunity" title="39382 Opportunity">39382 Opportunity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa12_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa12-28">[28]</a></sup> The name was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Ingrid_van_Houten-Groeneveld" title="Ingrid van Houten-Groeneveld">Ingrid van Houten-Groeneveld</a> who, along with <a href="/wiki/Cornelis_Johannes_van_Houten" title="Cornelis Johannes van Houten">Cornelis Johannes van Houten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Gehrels" title="Tom Gehrels">Tom Gehrels</a>, discovered the asteroid on September 24, 1960. Opportunity's lander is <i>Challenger Memorial Station</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChallengerMemorial_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChallengerMemorial-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 28, 2014, it was announced that <i>Opportunity</i>, having traversed over 40 km (25 mi), had become the rover achieving the longest off-world distance, surpassing the previous record of 39 km (24 mi) on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> by <a href="/wiki/Lunokhod_2" title="Lunokhod 2">Lunokhod 2</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140728a_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140728a-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRB-20140729_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FRB-20140729-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 24, 2015, NASA celebrated <i>Opportunity</i> having traveled the distance of a <a href="/wiki/Marathon" title="Marathon">Marathon</a> race, 42.195 kilometers, from the start of <i>Opportunity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s landing and travelling on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Images">Images</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Opportunity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Images">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The rover can take pictures with its different cameras, but only the PanCam camera has the ability to photograph a scene with different color filters. The panorama views are usually built up from PanCam images. As of November 20, 2013, <i>Opportunity</i> had returned 186,246 pictures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meridiani_Planum_PIA13704.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="2736" height="617" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Meridiani_Planum_PIA13704.jpg/750px-Meridiani_Planum_PIA13704.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Meridiani_Planum_PIA13704.jpg/1125px-Meridiani_Planum_PIA13704.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Meridiani_Planum_PIA13704.jpg/1500px-Meridiani_Planum_PIA13704.jpg 2x" width="750"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meridiani_Planum_PIA13704.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This map, color-coded for minerals (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CRISM" title="CRISM">CRISM</a>) and annotated, shows the rover's traverse up to about 2010 with some nearby features noted</div>
</div>
</div>

In May 2015 the rover visited what was dubbed Spirit of St. Louis Crater, a shallow crater about 110 feet (34 meters) long and 80 feet (24 meters) across. In its center is Lindbergh Mound, about 2-3 meters (yards) high. This version of the panorma is annotated and in <a href="/wiki/False_color" title="False color">false color</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></div>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars_Map.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Image_map" title="Image map">Interactive imagemap</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Mars" title="Geography of Mars">global topography of Mars</a>, overlain with <a href="/wiki/List_of_artificial_objects_on_Mars" title="List of artificial objects on Mars">locations of Mars landers and rovers</a> (Red label = <a href="/wiki/Mars_rover" title="Mars rover">Rover</a>; Blue label = <a href="/wiki/Mars_landing" title="Mars landing">Lander</a>; bold red/blue = currently active). Hover your mouse to see the names of over 25 <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Mars" title="Geography of Mars">prominent geographic features</a>, and click to link to them. Coloring of the base map indicates relative <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">elevations</a>, based on data from the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Laser_Altimeter" title="Mars Orbiter Laser Altimeter">Mars Orbiter Laser Altimeter</a> on NASA's <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a>. Reds and pinks are higher elevation (+3 km to +8 km); yellow is 0 km; greens and blues are lower elevation (down to −8 km). Whites (&gt;+12 km) and browns (&gt;+8 km) are the highest elevations. <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axes</a> are <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> and <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>; <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">Poles</a> are not shown.

</ul>
</li>
